  // Toggle answer mask on clicking the image
  var toggle = function() {
    var amask = document.getElementById('io-overlay');
    if (amask.style.display === 'block' || amask.style.display === '')
      amask.style.display = 'none';
    else
      amask.style.display = 'block'
  }
</script>

<script>
  // remove cloze syntax from revealed hint
  var hint = document.querySelector('.fullhint>[id^=""hint""]')
  var html = hint.innerHTML.replace(/\[\[oc(\d+)::(.*?)(::(.*?))?\]\]/mg, ""$2"")
  hint.innerHTML = html
  // scroll to cloze
  document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function() {
    setTimeout(function(){
      const cloze1 = document.getElementsByClassName(""cloze"")[0];
      const rect = cloze1.getBoundingClientRect();
      const absTop = rect.top + window.pageYOffset;
      const absBot = rect.bottom + window.pageYOffset;
      if (absBot >= window.innerHeight) {
        const height = rect.top - rect.bottom
        const middle = absTop - (window.innerHeight/2) - (height/2);
        window.scrollTo(0, middle);};
    }, 1);
  }, false);
</script>

Why do we need a semicolon before an self-invoked function?	"Two reasons: javascript uses parens mainly for order of operations in math, and for function declarations. By putting a ; in front of a self-invoked function we are forcing the interpreter to see the opening paren ( in front of the self-invoking function prevents it from becoming an argument to something that might precede it.<div><br></div><div>The other reason is that if you had two files with self-invoking functions, and concatenated those files together they would be all put onto the same line with no separator. Adding a semi-colon in front will keep a separator in btwn.<br><div><br></div><div>https://stackoverflow.com/questions/7365172/semicolon-before-self-invoking-function#:~:text=Putting%20a%20semicolon%20before%20the,become%20confused%20with%20function%20application.<br></div></div>"

What is an Ichimoku Cloud?	An alternative to the moving average.<br>But also much more than that. It attempts to calculate the support and resistance levels. It also shows trend momentum and direction.
What is an alternative to moving averages, and also shows support/resistance levels, trend direction and momentum.	Ichimoku Cloud
What is reactance?	In psychology, when someone feels threat from their autonomy or freedom being removed.<br><br>For example, when you tell someone what to do or what to believe or how to act.
What is the psychology term for when someone feels threatened because their feeling of autonomy/freedom of action/thought is being taken away?	Reactance&nbsp;
Example: I create an upwork job for setting up kafka. Then I immediately solve the issue by moving to RedPanda.	This is an example where when we feel like we have set in motion a solution, other solutions present themselves.<br><br>Why does this happen? Is this coincidence? Or what if by setting an alternative solution in motion it changes our psychology, allowing us to see alternative solutions? Or maybe it frees us up into a more-relaxed mind. Maybe it gives us a boost of motivation or energy that allows us to jump over an obstacle.<br><br>I am not sure. It might be impossible to know. However, a model can still be built around this concept, and learned, if there is a real pattern there, and not just a transient pattern in the noise, or a coincidence.

"__new__ is the first thing to run when we create a new instance of a class.<br><br>Then __init__ fires to initialize the object.<br><br>__repr__ gives a string-representation of the class.<br><br><br>(<a href=""https://realpython.com/python-class-constructor/"">https://realpython.com/python-class-constructor/</a>)"

What are some strange errors I might see when using vite? How do environment variables work in vite?	"They are loaded from the following files in my directory:<br>&nbsp;.env<br>&nbsp;.env.local<br>&nbsp;.env.[mode]<br>&nbsp;.env.[mode].local<br><br>They are exposed to the client source code via import.meta.env as strings.<br><br>Only env variablias prefixed with ""VITE_"" are loaded, in order to prevent accidental leaking to the client.<br><br>Vite also uses dotenv-expand."

VSCode launch vs attach?	Launch is a recipe for starting your app in debug mode before VSCode attaches to it. This is more familiar from the perspective of server and backend development. VSCode launches and sets up the processes for you.<br><br>Attach is a recipe for how to connect VS Code's debugger to an app or process that's already running. This is more familiar from the perspective of frontend development, for example attaching VSCode's debugger to a browser that is already running.

What is the difference between these two:<br><br>python __main__.py<br>python -m __main__.py<br><br>?	"The second command is telling python to run a module. Python will first look for a package named __main__ and then look for a module in that package named __main__.py.<br><br>""<span style=""color: rgb(55, 65, 81); background-color: rgb(247, 247, 248);"">This approach ensures that the module is properly set up as a package, and allows you to import other modules within the package using relative imports.""<br><br>The ""</span>python __main__.py"" command tells python to run the specified file directly. This is simpler, but not recommended for projects because you can't easily import other modules using relative imports.<span style=""color: rgb(55, 65, 81); background-color: rgb(247, 247, 248);""><br></span>"
How do you prevent a python file from executing more than once in your project?	Load it as a module. This will ensure that it is loaded once and cached.
Difference between the two:<br><div><div><br></div><div>from . import setupLogger<br><br><br></div></div><div>from .setupLogger import setupLogger</div><div><br></div>	The first one imports from the __init__.py module.<br><br>The second one will look in the setupLogger.py file in the current directory.<br><br>In the second one, setupLogger will be the function called setupLogger inside of setupLogger.py.<br><br>In the first one we will be importing everything inside of the setupLogger.py file, so in order to evoke the function we have to do setupLogger.setupLogger()
"Why does <br>`<span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">from</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);""> </span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">logger</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);""> </span><span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">import</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);""> </span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">logger`&nbsp;<br></span>run logger/__init__.py every time, and <br><br>`<span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">from</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);""> .</span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">add_project_root_to_path`</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;<br></span><span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">`import</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);""> </span><span style=""color: rgb(212, 212, 212);"">*&nbsp;</span><br><span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">from</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">src.logger</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(197, 134, 192);"">import</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">logger`&nbsp;<br></span>runs it only once?"	
"In python when you import a module using an absolute import it will execute the __init__.py file every time. When using a relative import it will only execute once.<br><br>So, I initially created a hack file called add_project_root_to_path.py.<br><br>Originally it looked like this (suggested by gpt):<br><br><div><div><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">this_dir</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;""> </span><span style=""color: #d4d4d4;"">=</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;""> </span><span style=""color: #4ec9b0;"">os</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">path</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">abspath</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #4ec9b0;"">os</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">path</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">dirname</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">__file__</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">))</span></div><br><div><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># Get the absolute path of the project root directory</span></div><div><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">project_root</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;""> </span><span style=""color: #d4d4d4;"">=</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;""> </span><span style=""color: #4ec9b0;"">os</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">path</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">abspath</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #4ec9b0;"">os</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">path</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">join</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">this_dir</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">, </span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">'..'</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">))</span></div><br><div><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># Add the project root directory to the system path</span></div><div><span style=""color: #4ec9b0;"">sys</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">path</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">.</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">insert</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #b5cea8;"">0</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">, </span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">project_root</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">)<br><br></span>The problem with this is that I had to do imports like from src.myModule, which worked. However, I could also do `from myModule` and it would still work, since src was in the project_root. So I ran into issues where I was mixing up these two ways of importing. And they looked similar. So I modified the second line to:<br><br><span style=""color: rgb(156, 220, 254);"">project_root</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(212, 212, 212);"">=</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">os</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">.</span><span style=""color: rgb(156, 220, 254);"">path</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">.</span><span style=""color: rgb(220, 220, 170);"">abspath</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">(</span><span style=""color: rgb(78, 201, 176);"">os</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">.</span><span style=""color: rgb(156, 220, 254);"">path</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">.</span><span style=""color: rgb(220, 220, 170);"">join</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">(</span><span style=""color: rgb(156, 220, 254);"">this_dir</span><span style=""color: rgb(255, 255, 255);"">,&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(206, 145, 120);"">'../..'<br></span><br>This forced me to have to do `from src.myModule` in order to import, enforcing that imports were always relative.<br></div></div>"	
What is a peerDependency?	It's defined in package.json.<br><br>It's a dependency that the project itself is not directly dependent upon. But other dependencies in the project are dependent on.<br><br>They are expected to be supplied by the consumer. That means when you run `yarn install` peerDependencies will not be installed. You will have to install them manually, or by running `yarn add --peer` or `npm install --peer`
What is a python namespace package, and when is it useful?	A namespace package gives you the ability to separate a package up into separate directories.<br><br>This is useful when the package is huge and you want to split it into pieces to make it more maintainable.&nbsp;<br><br>Some packages, such as numpy, also use this concept to create a common namespace for a group of related packages.

What is the Dilution Effect?	When you make an argument or present examples or evidence. The listener takes an average of all of the examples.<br><br>For example, good arguments mixed with weak arguments undermines the good arguments.
[tech]<br><br>What does `cross-env NODE_ENV=custom-env-vars mocha test/ --recursive --exit` do?	It uses cross-env, which sets the environment variables, which gets them from custom-env-vars.env, and then uses mocha to look inside the test/ folder, using the recursive option to look for test files in the subdirectories of test/. Finally it exits the process once finished.

[tech] [feathersjs]<br><br>What is an external resolver in feathers?	A normal resolver is both for the data coming into feathers and the data going out.<br><br>An external resolver is only for data going out of feathers to the client. It is useful for hiding sensitive data. For example, you sent back the api response to a user but hide certain data. But it's usually used for highly sensitive data, such as passwords.

Pros and cons of having statusMngr for every OP, SP, vs having a master statusManager object.	If I have a master statusManager object then this might be better because it is decoupling the statusManager from the OP and SP.<br><br>Rather than having a architecture where everything is neatly nested together I would have a flatter structure, that is more flexible and less error-prone, easier to change, etc.
Pros and cons of having SubProcedures underneath OrderProcedures vs having them both spawned in the src/index.js file, and then linked together?	If I keep everything in the src/index.js file then it leads to a flatter architecture. Then I don't need to worry about bubbling events, or accessing variables in a nested way, etc, etc.<br><br>I think, in general, a flatter structure is easier to keep and separate. It's also easier for the debugger to work and for 3rd-party libraries to hook into my code.<br><br>The most nested by structure is (think folders vs tags) the harder in the future it will become to change things. Every change will casuse a cascade of changes throughout my entire application.&nbsp;
Why can't we have a flat architecture for OPs and SPs?	Because the OPs need to take the input from the SPs and make the final order decisions, needs to be smart and determine if orders should cancel out each other, etc.<br><br>So SP needs to be underneath OP.
Reflect on the pros and cons of a flat vs a nested architecture 	
Three tick architectures?<br><br>Pros & Cons?<br><br>Reflect.	1. Index ticks OPs. OP ticks its SPs.<br><br>2. Index ticks OPs and SPs.<br><br>3. OPs and SPs are responsible for themselves, and set a timeout after running and then call themselves.
Massage spine from bottom-up and top-down.	Why?<br><br>Because if the spine is sensitive then it will try to stabilize from the sources of trauma, impact, force generation.<br><br>Tonnes of that force will come through walking, running up from the hips.<br><br>Remember: the issues could start from the shoulders, sensitizing the spine, And then the spine might start locking up from the bottom-up due to the incredible forces from running, walking, with a backpack, etc.<br><br>

[tech]<br><br><div><br>  <h3>Fix this script:</h3><br>  <pre><br>    <code><br>const mochaScripts = Object.entries(packageJSONscripts)<br>  .filter(([key, val]) => packageJSONscripts[key].includes('mocha'))<br>  .map(([key, val]) => ({ key: val }));<br>    </code><br>  </pre><br></div>	<div><br>  <h3>Fixed Script:</h3><br>  <pre><br>    <code><br>const mochaScripts = Object.entries(packageJSONscripts)<br>  .filter(([key, val]) =&gt; val.includes('mocha'))<br>  .map(([key, val]) =&gt; ({ [key]: val }));<br>    </code><br>  </pre><br>  <br>  <h3>Explanation:</h3><br>  <ul><br>    <li>Removed unnecessary repetition of <code>packageJSONscripts[key]</code>, as <code>val</code> already holds the value.</li><br>    <li>Ensured the <code>map()</code> method correctly creates objects with <code>key</code> as the property name (using <code>[key]</code>) and <code>val</code> as the value.</li><br>    <li><code>[key]</code> dynamically sets the property name in the object to the value of the variable <code>key</code>.</li><br>  </ul><br>  <br>  <h3>Notes for Better Understanding:</h3><br>  <ul><br>    <li><code>Object.entries()</code> converts an object into an array of <code>[key, value]</code> pairs.</li><br>    <li>The <code>filter()</code> method narrows down entries to those containing <code>'mocha'</code> in their value.</li><br>    <li><code>[key]: val</code> dynamically sets the property name in the resulting object, making the object structure <code>{ scriptName: scriptValue }</code>.</li><br>  </ul><br></div>

Why do I usually need to create an async init function for stuff requiring the file system?	Because the file system is slow, we can't write or read from it very efficiently. So fs supplies a lot of different ways to deal with files and dirs. Because of the delay, if I am going to create an object that deals with files then I need to usually await it to initialize. This requires an init() function.

Look at your programs through the lens of determinism vs chaotic. During moments of chaos of the program it's good to taper things down every now and then. It's similar to having a giant factory. <br><br>Some parts of the process are extremely volatile, high temperature, dangerous, etc. If you can't pull apart the pieces and separate out a lot of it you have to build a chamber around it. Instead of one big chamber, try to turn it into a series of smaller ones.<br><br>It's a strange imagery, but imagine a program starting with very low randomness, and throughout its life there are moments where randomness spikes. Volatility spikes and you have to program around that.<br><br>You have to test in a different way. It's good to use things like state, good logging, assertions, etc during these moments to make it easy to backtrack from errors when they happen.

"""In general, if you want to check conditions that could be violated due to external factors (like bad input from a user or a file), throwing errors after manual checks is a good choice. If you want to just fetch the headers of a resource.<br><br>This is useful, for example, maybe you don't know if the resource exists or not, or you want to know its size so you know how to deal with it before fetching the whole resource.<br><br>For example, this is also useful if I don't know whether an image exists or not. What is Galileo's Principle?	A system that is highly coupled and complex will fail and fail hard.<br><br>Every time you add something new that's a new layer of complexity.<br><br>If you have a beam resting on 2 supports you might think 3 supports is better. But it's likely that the middle support could take on more weight than the ends and end up cracking the beam.

"When to use if __name__ == ""__main__"" ?"	"<div>It is commonly used in Python scripts to differentiate between code that should be executed when the script is run directly and code that should only be executed when the script is imported as a module into another Python script.</div><div>When you use the <code>if __name__ == ""__main__"":</code> block, the code inside it will only run if the Python script is executed directly from the command line. It won't run if the script is imported as a module in another Python script. This can be useful when you want to include some initial setup or testing code that should only be executed when running the script as a standalone program.</div><br>"

Python: What is the downside of importing a python file/function by adding to sys.path?	<ol><li><div>Dependency Management: Adding a path to <code>sys.path</code> may bypass the standard Python package management system (e.g., <code>pip</code> and <code>virtualenv</code>), leading to dependency conflicts or version issues. It could be challenging to manage different versions of the same package required by different modules or projects.</div></li><li><div>Code Maintainability: Modifying <code>sys.path</code> to import modules can make the code less readable and harder to maintain. It might introduce confusion about the origin of modules, especially in larger projects.</div></li><li><div>Global Scope: Modifying <code>sys.path</code> affects the global Python environment, making it possible for other parts of the code to unintentionally access modules from the added path, potentially leading to unexpected behavior.</div></li><li><div>Import Order: The order in which paths are added to <code>sys.path</code> matters. If multiple directories have modules with the same name, the Python interpreter will import the first one found, which might not be the intended module.</div></li><li><div>Portability: Hardcoding paths in the code makes it less portable and can cause issues when moving the code to a different environment or sharing it with others.</div></li><li><div>Security Risks: If the path being added contains untrusted or user-controlled data, it could lead to security vulnerabilities, such as code injection attacks.</div></li></ol><br>

Why is it a good idea to have a setupLogger file in each module/workspace, even if you have something like @ktr-srt/logging setup? What is the difference between these two:<br><br>git reset<br><br>git reset --hard	"`git reset` allows you to move the branch pointer to a specific commit. This effectively ""uncommits"" changes. <br><br>By default, it moves the branch pointer to the specified commit but leaves your working directory and staging area unchanged.<br><br>This command is often used to uncommit changes while preserving the changes in your working directory. You can then recommit, amend, or modify the changes before committing.<br><br><br>`git reset --hard` also updates both the working directory and staging area.<br>Any uncommited changes in your working directory and staging area are discarded.<br><br><br>https://stackoverflow.com/questions/3639342/whats-the-difference-between-git-reset-and-git-checkout"

What is ** in python?<br><br>For example:<br><br>schema = [bigquery.SchemaField(**col) for col in schema_json]	Unpacking dicts.<br><br>Note: To unpack strings, lists, tuples, sets use a single *.
Reflect/Ponder: If an id is only digits should it be store as a NUMERIC or as a STRING?	The arguments for it being stored as NUMERIC over a STRING are:<br><br>Dealing with NUMERICS are easier for the database, and are easier to store and smaller and easier to work with.<br><br>The other argument is more of an information theory analogy. The container/schema of a piece of data should be flexible but not overly open. It should be as tight as possible to the data, without too much room for interpretation.<br><br>For example, in one of my projects in python I am using BigQuery and csv data. The question was, should id be NUMERIC or STRING? The problem is that just by looking at the csv file you can't tell. You would have to store the types in the header, or look at the database schema. This gets confusing. If it was just stored as a NUMERIC then all you have to do is look at the data itself and you can determine the type by iterating through all rows, and if only a single row has a non-digit in the id then the type should be STRING.<br><br>

postgres: When inserting bulk data.. what happens if one row has a unique contraint error?	The entire operation is rolled back and no rows are added into the table.<br><br>This behaviour can be overridded by using `ON CONFLICT`.<br><br>But this is proably not a good thing to do. The purpose behind this behaviour is that you don't want to leave a database in an in-between state. You want the entire operation to finish, or be rolled back.<br><br>This makes debugging much easier.. Imagine if a bunch of rows were conflicting but you didn't get an alert. It's much easier if the entire operation failed and had to be rolled back. Then you can debug, and the database will be back into the state is started in before the operation.<br><br>Remember: databases are heavy and each operation should be carefully considered. That's why it's better to be careful when moving from one state to another.

How to treat IT band syndrome?<br><br>$addToCard:(Add other examples of things the answer treats / is good for)	Do different exercises while keeping the pelvis level.<br><br>Then progress from skill, endurance, strength, then plymotrics and training on uneven surfaces.<br>
Which part of piriformis should be treated?	Distal piriformis is mostly tendon. In order to treat, massage the piriformis focus on the proximal section of the muscle, connecting to the sacrum.
Which muscle doesn't have a tendon?<br><br>$addToCard:(are there others?)	Adductor magnus
What is happening when muscles start to shake?<br><br>For example doing a 45 degree angle single glute bridge, and keeping the contralateral leg extended and the pelvis up until shaking in the quads.	Slow twitch fibers to fast twitch fibers.<br><br>One motor nerve goes to around 2,000 fibers. So it's not as coordinated. In slow twitch fibers it's 200-300 fibers. <br><br>That's why you see the shake.
Why do the worlds best runners have longer Achilles Tendons?	Because tendons are good sprint force generators.<br><br>On heel-strike they will shorten and then on toe-off they will lengthen like a spring and push off and generate force to propel the leg forward.
Why does the tibia rotate the same whether or not the talus rotates more or less, which will change depending on whether you have a high arch or not?	Because the sub-talus joint is a torsion dissipator. So it absorbs that rotational force while the foot is on the ground during the gait cycle.<br><br>High-arch: will evert 4 degrees, but tibia will turn in 8 degrees.<br><br>Pronator: will evert 10 degrees, but tibia will only turn in 8 degrees.<br><br><br>Source: (YouTube) Clinical Servant Series, Dr Tom Michaud , Human Locomotion Part 1
"Evolutionary purpose of piriformis?<br><br>(And lower fibers of glute medius)<img src=""1000017719.jpg"">"	You would think that because the femoral head is at a large angle between the acetabulum then there would be a lot of stress along the neck of the femur.<br><br>This would lead to a thickening of cortical bone. <br><br>But this doesn't happen. The piriformis and lower fibers of the glute medius counteract these forces during the gait cycle.<br><br><br>Note: cortical bone refers to the cortex of the bone, which is the outer part.<br><br><br><br><br>
Why is running so efficient and hard to burn calories with?	When you walk you isometrically contract a lot of muscles. This allows the tendons to generate a lot of the force instead of the muscles and is why walking is so efficient and hard to burn calories with.<br><br>
What was the most likely reason for rapid brain expansion during evolution?	Fire.<br><br>This allowed us to extract more calories from our food, and sleep.<br><br>Sleep led to REM sleep, which leads to rapid brain development.
[physio]<br><br>Why should toe raises be performed at the top height of the toe raise?	Because the function of the toe flexors is at the top of that range just before toe-off. So we need to train the muscles at that length, where they execute during that phase of the gait cycle.<br><br>This is why the toe pro is angled, so that when you do heel raises your toes will be in an extended position, mimicking toe-off.

Visualize what happens after injuring a single joint or vertebrae.	On injured discs or joints the area loses its stability or ability to stabilize.<br><br>This means other parts around have to attempt to stabilize this area, or this might be the part of the spine where most of the stress goes into during a certain motion, or overhead lift, etc<br><br>Imagine you can stiffen up all vertebrae except for one. So in order for a muscle to absorb force, whether or not it is an eccentric or concentric muscle, it has to be tight enough to match the tightness coming from the other side.<br><br>If the tightness of one guy wire on one side of a tower is tighter than one on the opposing side then the one on the opposing side will be loose, and therefore will not be able to transmit, transfer, absorb or attenuate any forces.

if I can't do camelCase then use _ and __.	helloWorld becomes hello_world<br>&amp; adReport_helloWorld becomes ad_report__hello_world<br><br>This way I can group things together. For example, if I wanted to do c_tableName_addHashColumn, but unfortunately the table name is using underscores. For example, my_table.csv: then we would need to expant out tableName into table_name. But c_table_name_addHashColumn is ugly.<br><br>So it should be c__table_name__addHashColumn<br>OR c__table_name__add_hash_column.<br><br>Unfortunately this doesn't solve the problem of when to use camelCase and when to use underscores. Typically, my philiosophy is use to camelCase because it feels like it groups things together more intimately. Underscores are for spacing things out. The further words are from each other, the less connection they feel to have.<br><br><br>P.S. I think I can think of dashes - as something sometimes synonymous with slashes /.&nbsp;<br>So slashes and dashes are weird because they are dividing things, but at the same time dashes feel different that underscores They feel like the words are closer than underscores, but maybe I should start thinking of them as something that's similar to slashes, which are useful for filenames, since we know that dashes can be placeholders for slashes without creating problems or having to escape characters.
"<img src=""Screenshot 2023-11-30 at 7.21.49 PM.png""><br><br>Is it good to have an __init__.py file for each utility directory?"	"<img src=""Screenshot 2023-11-30 at 7.21.54 PM.png""><br><br>The value of doing so allows you to import it like so:<br>from util.dirName import subFunction.<br><br>The cost of doing so is when you want to move the files around, you will have to change a lot of things. VSCode will automatically update references to the file path, but if I drag util/myFile.py into util/myDir then I will also have to edit util/myDir/__init__.py. This makes it heavy and difficult to play around with the folder and file structure.<br><br>If we leave everything in util/__init__.py and don't have __init__ files inside each subDir then it will be much more lightweight. And we can still import things like `from util import anything.py`<br><br>This makes a lot more sense"
The main rule for formatting?	The main rule for formatting:<br><br>if you want to be able to line up information, for example filenames, then put them at the beginning of the logs.<br><br>This is because the closer to the beginning of each log entry, the more likely it will be lined up with the lines above and below it.<br><br>Put details, etc towards the end.<br><br><br>But when to use color? Use color to differentiate different log levels, but only color warn, info, etc if possible.

Why is `poetry` a bad name for a project directory?	Because it doesn't mean anything out-of-context.<br><br>It's better to add as much context to a name as possible, and then start to consolidate into a better name over time.<br><br>This is better because it preserves meaning. Whereas `poetry` only has meaning as a project directory name if seen in context, ie lightboxmediagroup/poetry, something like poetry-reporting, in order to distinguish it from the original, older project, is a better name.<br><br>Over time, the meanings and relations between things will evolve, and so will the name.

Differences between pytest and unittest.	"Pytest has simpler, more powerful discovery:<br><br>`pytest .` will automatically search all subdirs for ""test_*.py"" files.<br><br>unittest requires something like `python -m unittest discover -s ""."" -p ""test_*.py""`<br>and it doesn't recurse, and you can only add one directory with the -s command.<br><br>Also, you have to name the test folder something like test_mypackage. If you don't and you are testing multiple packages all with their test folders named ""test"" then in the TEST panel in vscode is will just show test, test, test, etc.<br>It won't tell you which is which."

"When to use absolute paths, and relative paths?<br><br># think # reflect # addTo"	"It's better to pass around absolute paths instead of relative. If we are passing around relative paths then we have to ask, ""relative to what?"", the project root, or the calling file, or from the cwd? Project-relative paths?<br><br>This might not be a super difficult question to answer but it adds a lot of mental energy, reducing the amount that can be successfully juggled at one time in one's mind.<br><br>Relative folders are great for accessing things in the beginning. For example, config files, env files, or getting a file from a data directory. This is because if we change the folder structure around things will still work. But it seems after that it's much better to use absolute paths. Relative paths can be created easily. All we have to do is strip out everything up to the project directory. Going in the other direction is more complex. And you don't want to have too much complexity at the top of your functions.<br><br>The top of functions should be reserved for checking the argument inputs, checking side effects, etc, etc. Giving the function a good summary. Similar to how the first sentence in a paragraph gives an overview of what the paragraph will be about.<br><br>Ask self: ""Do we need relative paths in this situation?"" If not, then the answer is to probably use absolute paths. They are lightweight. Moving around lightweight things is much less complex."
[tech]<br><br>When deciding whether to use absolute paths, relative paths, or project-relative paths,<br>Or when making other types of decisions on how to pass things around.. ie data types, etc.<br><br>What can we do? There are so many different ways to go about things..	- Look at other, similar libraries that deal with a similar type of data and see how they do it.<br>&nbsp; &nbsp; i.e. when dealing with files or filepaths, look at other libraries and see what they accept as argument input, what they return, etc<br><br>- think about things in terms of complexity. An unstable ball is easy to manage. An unstable ball on top of another unstable ball is another thing entirely. You can't build vertically until there is enough stability underneath.<br><br>- learn about things horizontally in order to have enough knowledge to create a landscape in your head. Once you have this then you can lay down pros &amp; cons that you read or exprience on top of this landscape in your head. Without something like this, it's hard to lay memories down because you won't know where, and when or how to retrieve them when needed.&nbsp;<br>&nbsp; - Often times I will spend days programming something only to find out it adds too much complexity for it to be worth it. Even though I had that thought from the beginning. But sometimes you also want to learn things enough (by doing/buiding) before you feel comfortable enough horizontally before you can make and stick with your decision. Another example might be whether to use underscores or camelCase, and then you end up mixing the 2.

"<span style=""font-weight: 600;"">What does this mean?<br><br>python = ""^3.7""</span>"	This means that the project supports python versions 3.7, and up to (but not including) the next major release.<br><br>So in this case it means that 3.7, and everything up until, but not including 4.0.0 is supported.
"What does this mean?<br><br><span style=""font-weight: 600;"">python = ""^3.7,^3.11.4""</span>"	This allows your project to work with Python versions ranging from <code>3.7</code> up to, but not including, <code>4.0.0</code>, while being more specific about supporting at least Python <code>3.11.4</code>. This is useful when you want to ensure compatibility with a specific minimum version of Python but also want to be flexible enough to work with newer minor versions within the same major version.

"<span style=""color: rgb(236, 236, 241); background-color: rgb(52, 53, 65);"">&nbsp; File ""/Users/luketych/.vscode/extensions/ms-python.python-2024.0.1/pythonFiles/lib/python/debugpy/_vendored/pydevd/pydevd.py"", line 3244
    def log_to(log_file:str, log_level=3) -&gt; None:
                       ^
SyntaxError: invalid syntax<br></span><br>What does this error indicate?"	"The wrong version of python is being used.<br><br>In my lbmg poetry project this was because my execute_script functions were using ""python"" instead of sys.executable. This was, for some reason, using the wrong version of python, and not the one that was installed and activated via poetry.<br><br>Thischatgpt offers the following explanations:<br><br><ol><li><div><strong>Path Resolution</strong>: When you use <code>""python""</code> directly, it relies on the system's PATH environment variable to find the Python executable. If the PATH is set to a directory containing a different Python interpreter before the one managed by Poetry, it might use that instead. In contrast, <code>sys.executable</code> provides the absolute path to the Python interpreter, avoiding reliance on the PATH.</div></li><li><div><strong>Activation Script</strong>: Poetry typically sets up the virtual environment and modifies the PATH variable or provides an activation script to ensure that the correct Python interpreter is used when you run commands. If you're not using an activation script or it's not being sourced correctly in your environment, it might lead to the wrong Python version being used.</div></li><li><div><strong>Shell Interpolation</strong>: When you pass <code>""python""</code> directly to <code>subprocess.run()</code>, the shell might perform additional interpretation or substitution before executing the command, potentially leading to unexpected behavior. Using <code>sys.executable</code> bypasses the shell altogether and directly executes the specified Python interpreter.</div></li></ol>"

[python]<br><br>in Python logging, what is a transport called?	destination
Explain the concepts of Decorator, Formatter, Wrapper	First we use the Wrapper. The wrapper's job is simply to set up the logger, ie winston, or pino. It sets the formatter, transports/destinations (or handlers in python), and it implements the functions for debug, error, info, fatal, trace, warn. It also (optionally) calls the stack in order to find the caller lineNum and colNum.<br><br>Finally, it calls the Decorator.<br><br>Decorators are files that house the debug, error, info, etc functions. They should be simple. One implementation is to simply create a new object called a metaObj and attach to it the caller, tags, options and the loggableObject, then call&nbsp;this.logger.info(loggableObjMeta).<br><br>In the past I would format things here but this got messy. After the decortor returns it will call the formatter.<br><br>Formatters: depending on which formatters are set in the wrapper, (or possibly passed in through options) the corresponding format function can be called. I like to have a basicFormatter, and basicString formatter, a flattenObject formatter, etc. This is where some of the more complex logic can be housed. This is good because it's also at the end of the logging pipeline, and so the complexity or errors do not cause more errors to be pushed further down the pipeline.
"[tech]<br><br>What are your thoughts surrounding this?<br><br>test_resources_path = os.path.join(""tests"", ""resources"")"	"- it's not debuggable/searchable. If I have an error that contains ""tests/resources"", which is what will appear in the logs/terminal, it will be difficult to search for this error.<br><br>@learn @lesson @habit<br>Become aware of how each line I code may present itself at the endpoints/extremities (ie console, log file). How will this affect debugging?<br><br>@habit<br>Develop the habit of searching for things in differnent way, for example, if I see ""tests/resources"" then maybe there could be a line that looks similar, but is not assembled into the final string yet.<br><br>Things become difficult to debug when there is no line where we can see ""tests/resources"" directly. Maybe I need to make a habit that before anything gets finalized on one layer (ie my code) and passed onto the next (potentially logging, or some other module), the data passed should be guaranteed to be passed in a searchable, and easy-to-handle format.<br><br>As a habit/rule, whenever passing things from one layer to another, or one module to another, or from one form to another ALWAYS make sure that the format is a good one and easy to debug and find.<br><br>If, for example, I am passing data from one side of an api to another, on the sending end (if I control it) I should make sure that I format the data in a searchable, debuggable way. On the receiving end I should take the data and confirm that it's a good format, or reformat it as an early step. Or at least create a new meta object, add it to the meta object, and then add other data that indicates that the object needs to be reformatted. Maybe then I could pass it to a parser or processor before dealing with it further.<br><br>This seems like a good habit to cultivate."
It looks like in python I cannot run an individual file in the debugger.<br>&nbsp;<br>This is because then if the file ever tries to do a relative import, ie `from getProjectRootDir import getProjectRootDir` it will fail.<br><br>This is because python would need to import this as a module in order to have access to relative imports.	** This might actually be a good thing. It will force me to test anything I write through a tests/ directory, instead of inside of the file itself. This is good practice and will help to re-enfore good habits.

[tech]<br>[poetry]<br><br> what are ~= and ^ called? For example, ~=1.2.3	Dependency version specifiers.
[tech]<br>[python package management]<br>[poetry]<br><br>What kind of dependency version specifier is this?&nbsp;<strong><code>~=1.2.3<br></code></strong><br>What does it mean?	<b>compatible release</b> specifier.<br><br>~=1.2.3 means you can install any version >= 1.2.3 & < 1.3.0<br><br><b>This is similar to caret specifiers used in JavaScript, node, rust. They have small differences but mostly the same.</b>
[tech]<br><br>What kind of dependency version specifier is this? ^<strong><code>1.2.3<br></code></strong><br>What does it mean?	Caret specifier.<br><br>It means it is compatible with version 1.2.3 and any other backward-compatible versions up to, but not including, the next major release.<br><br>It is similar to ~= but a bit more flexible. It allows installing patch and minor releases that are backwar-compatible.<br><br><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><div><strong>Example</strong>: Let's say your project specifies a dependency with <code>^1.2.3</code>. Here's what it implies:</div><ul><li>You're okay with installing version 1.2.3 of the dependency.</li><li>You're also okay with installing any version within the same major release line (1.x.x) as long as it's backward-compatible with 1.2.3.</li><li>You won't automatically get version 2.0.0 or higher because those could potentially introduce breaking changes.</li></ul><div>For example, with <code>^1.2.3</code>, you might get versions like <code>1.2.4</code>, <code>1.3.0</code>, or <code>1.9.9</code>, but you won't get versions like <code>2.0.0</code> or <code>3.0.0</code> automatically. This provides a balance between stability and the availability of bug fixes and new features.</div></div></div></div><div><div><div></div><div></div></div></div><div></div></div><div><div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div><div><div></div><div><br></div></div></div>
Does poetry automate the generation of version specifiers?	yes

[tech]<br><br>Mutable vs Immutable data types?	Immutable data types cannot change once created. For example, tuples in python. In javascript we have to do things like Object.seal(), Object.freeze(), const and other libraries like immutable.js.<br><br>The value of immutable data types are that:<br><br>- we don't have side effects.<br><br>- They are easier to predict and optimize. <br><br>- They are also better for threads, since we know there won't be side effects.<br><br>- They are better-used as hashes. Imagine we use a mutable object as a hash. If that changes, then the hash would not, which would make things confusing.
[tech]<br><br>If my database is using hashes instead of IDs what do I do if I update a row, and the data that makes up the hash gets altered?	Generate a new hash.
Should you use a ID or a hash or both in the database?	Hashes are good because they can be compared more easily. For example, we can look at the hashes between different rows and see how they differ.<br>Hashes are also good to ensure data integrity. We can rehash the values to make sure they match the expected hash, which is already stored.<br>Hashes are also useful for synchronization. We can quickly &amp; easily identify rows that have changed and need to be sync'ed.&nbsp;<br><br>IDs are good because they rarely, if ever, change.<br>IDs are useful for establishing and managing relationships between tables (foreign keys.)<br>IDs are generally numeric, making it easy and fast to work with them.<br><br><br>Advantages of using both:<br>- Versioning and History:&nbsp; We can store multiple versions of a row, each with the same ID, but wish different hashes that change over time.<br><br>However, this might be more difficult to manage.
Should IDs be numeric, or string?	Numeric IDs are easier to work with for computers, easier to calculate and sort, auto-increment.<br><br>However string-based IDs are more universal: they can work for numeric IDs and UUIDs and IDs from other sources.<br><br>Numeric IDs are more efficient and suitable for sorting and range queries. For example, if you routinely want to find all rows of data within a certain range of IDs.

This is likely the cause, and the reason why restarting vscode is becuase vscode will go through this step on setting up.

For example, it might put a 0, when you wanted a 0.0.<br><br>psychpg2 retrieves numerical types as Decimal objects by default.<br>(This means that if I retrieve data from my neon table then floats will be converted to Decimals.)
Difference between float and Decimal in python?	float is a built-in data type. Decimal is a python module.<br><br>float using floating-point arithmetic, meaning that it approximates 0.1 as (0.0001100110011001100110011001100110011001100110011001101...)<br><br>Whereas, a Decimal will store it the way a human reads it: ('0.1')

[tech]<br><br>Think about different testing methodologies:<br><br><b>The Ice Cream Cone</b><br>Vs<br><b>The Pyramid</b>	"<img src=""softwaretestingicecreamconeantipattern.png""><br><br><i>With a software testing ice cream cones, the majority of testing is done manually. UI automated test are a close second, integration test in the middle, with unit testing lagging behind completely. This is not scalable.</i><br><br><img src=""eye.jpg""><br><br>    <p>The <strong>Ice Cream Cone Anti-Pattern</strong> in software testing prioritizes manual and end-to-end (E2E) tests over unit and integration tests, inverting the efficient Testing Pyramid.</p><br><br>    <b>Characteristics</b><br>    <ul><br>        <li>Heavy reliance on slow, fragile manual and UI/E2E tests.</li><br>        <li>Few unit and integration tests, missing early defect detection.</li><br>        <li>Centralized QA, siloed teams, sequential workflows.</li><br>    </ul><br><br>    <b>Drawbacks</b><br>    <ul><br>        <li>High maintenance costs for E2E tests.</li><br>        <li>Slow feedback loops delay releases.</li><br>        <li>Late bug detection increases fix costs.</li><br>        <li>Fragile tests break with UI changes.</li><br>        <li>Unscalable as features grow.</li><br>    </ul><br><br>    <b>Causes</b><br>    <ul><br>        <li>Initial ease of manual/UI testing.</li><br>        <li>Lack of unit testing expertise.</li><br>        <li>Siloed teams or legacy codebases.</li><br>        <li>Short-term focus over strategic automation.</li><br>    </ul><br><br>    <b>Avoidance Strategies</b><br>    <ul><br>        <li>Prioritize unit and integration tests (e.g., via TDD/BDD).</li><br>        <li>Automate low-level tests, limit E2E to critical paths.</li><br>        <li>Foster developer-QA collaboration.</li><br>        <li>Use CI for frequent, fast test runs.</li><br>        <li>Limit manual testing to exploratory cases.</li><br>    </ul><br><br>"

What does OLTP stand for, and what is its primary purpose?	"OLTP stands for ""Online Transaction Processing.""<br><br>It's main purpose is to handle a large number of short transactions (insert, update, delete,)&nbsp;"
What does OLAP stand for, and what is it's primary purpose?	"OLAP stands for ""Online Analytical Processing.""<br><br>It's main purpose is to analyze large amount os data quickly. It supports compelx queries, data discovery, and decision-making processes."
What are some examples of OLTP systems?	Mongo<br>MySQL<br>Postgres<br>Redis
What are some OLAP systems?	RedShift<br>BigQuery<br>SnowFlake<br>DuckDB
Is DuckDB OLAP or OLTP?	OLAP

"[tech]<br><br><div><div><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">What is the correct way to parse this env variable inside of our program?</span></div><div><span style=""color: #569cd6;""><br></span></div><div><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">.env:</span></div><div><span style=""color: #569cd6;""><br></span></div><div><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">__KTR__TABLE_IDS</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">=</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">'ad_history'</span></div><div><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># ad_report_daily,</span></div><div><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># advertiser</span></div></div><div><br></div>"	"<span style=""color: rgb(124, 166, 104);"">Wrong: ENV_VARS[""TABLE_IDS""]<br></span><br>Correct: ENV_VARS[""TABLE_IDS""].split("","")<br><br>This works, because if there are multiple rows of data defined in the .env file for TABLE_IDS then it will be correctly parsed into an array. If there is only a single value, we still should parse it as an array, as that is what is expected by our progam."
Why might it be a good idea to name my id's like _id or ktr_id, or _id_ktr instead of simply id?	Because it makes it less likely to avoid potential conflicts.<br><br>Example:<br><br>the tiktok.advertiser table there is a column already called `id`. So in my pipeline when I tried to add a id column it was conflicting. If, instead, I called my id column _id, there would be no conflict. Then later on down the pipeline I can clean up all of the names.<br><br>Lesson:<br><br>The more unique you name things the less likely there might be conflicts. This is in opposition to following convention. A downside of following all conventions like this is that you might run into conflicts when dealing with data from different sources.

[physio]<br><br>What happens to the afferent and efferent signaling when at the end range of motion?	"The nervous system must send more information to the muscles.<br><br><br>Maybe this is because the nerve may be stretched and therefore information is harder to send through, requiring the nervous system to overcompensate with some sort of ""pressure"" increase.<br><br>Does this require more blood?<br><br>Muscles are also outside of their optimal ranges and mechanoreceptors are less accurate. Muscles aren't as ""strong"". Movement becomes more twitchy and less precise. Compensation might come from a greater recruitment of other muscle fibers."

Difference between mylist.extend() and mylist.append()?	mylist.append([]) will add the list[] as an element to mylist. The end result will be a list, with a list as an element.<br><br>mylist.extend([]) will take the elements from the list[] and add it to itself as elements. The end result will be a list. Not a list of lists.
Git scenario: I am currently checking out branch develop. I decide to checkout the branch red-box. For some reason, I now have folders that exist in the develop branch, but shouldn't be in the red-box branch. But I see both.<br><br>What to do?	"`git status` to see which files are currently untracked.<br><br>`git clean -ndx` to see which files would be deleted.<br><div>&nbsp; &nbsp;  -n (or --dry-run): Show what would be done without actually doing it.</div>
<div> &nbsp; &nbsp;  -d: Remove untracked directories in addition to untracked files.</div>
<div> &nbsp; &nbsp;  -x: Remove all untracked files, including those ignored by .gitignore.</div><br>If all else fails:<br><br>git fetch origin<br>git checkout red-box<br>git reset --hard origin/red-box<br>git clean -fdx<br><br><div><b>Explanation:</b></div>
<div><br></div>
<div>  git fetch origin updates your local copy of the remote repositorys branches.</div>
<div>  git checkout red-box switches to the red-box branch.</div>
<div>  git reset --hard origin/red-box resets your local branch to exactly match the remote branch, discarding any local changes and commits.</div>
<div>  git clean -fdx removes all untracked files and directories, ensuring that your working directory is clean and only contains files tracked by Git.</div>"
What does the following command do?<br><br><div>git branch -f my-branch my-tag</div>	It will force the branch my-branch to point to the tag my-tag.
How to point branch my-branch to tag my-tag?	git branch -f my-branch my-tag

How to add a range of commits to a specific branch?	git checkout &lt;branch&gt;<br>git cherry-pick &lt;commit-hash-a&gt;^..&lt;commit-hash-b&gt;
How do I get a neat, tidy git log?	git log --oneline

[physio]<br>[[think]] [[reflect]]<br><br>What position is worst for the spine when working?	Flexed/rounded because when the spine is extended then all vertebrae are locked and stable.<br><br>When rounded they are all unstable and can shift and rotate easily. So if you have bad habits and are twisting or leaning then it will lead to a lot of problems.<br><br>A flexed spine is good and natural for certain lifting, movements. But under a constant long duration of sitting with a flexed position we have to be aware of the strain being put on certain tissues.

&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; print</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">colored</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">f</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">""Warning: The script output for </span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">{</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">script_name</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">}</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;""> contains an error.""</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">, </span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">""yellow""</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">)) </span><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># yellow for warning</span></div><div><span style=""color: #ffffff;""></span><span style=""color: #c586c0;"">&nbsp; &nbsp; else:</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">:</span></div><div><span style=""color: #ffffff;""></span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; print</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #dcdcaa;"">colored</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">(</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">f</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">""Script </span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">{</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">script_parent_dir</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">}</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">/</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">{</span><span style=""color: #9cdcfe;"">script_name</span><span style=""color: #569cd6;"">}</span><span style=""color: #ce9178;""> completed successfully""</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">, </span><span style=""color: #ce9178;"">""green""</span><span style=""color: #ffffff;"">)) </span><span style=""color: #7ca668;""># Green for success</span></div>"	"Because sometimes I get weird warnings like the following:<br><br>```<br>DeprecationWarning: Accessing `SchemaFieldNotFoundError` from the top-level `polars` module is deprecated. Import it directly from the `polars.exceptions` module instead: from polars.exceptions import SchemaFieldNotFoundError<br>&nbsp; except pl.SchemaFieldNotFoundError as e:<br>```<br><br>This then get's caught because it contains the keyword ""error"" and it leads to an actual error.<br><br>In general it's bad to work with strings and have to read them and interpret them. It's difficult when it comes to logging, which is why it's better to use structured logging and stay in a json or other structured format for as long as possible and only use strings at the endpoints.<br>And it's why it's a bad idea to use the task_wrapper system I set up earlier, because it depends on reading error codes and return codes, which don't always exist and aren't reliable, and then forcing me to read the returned strings, parsing them, etc, which leads down all sorts of rabbit holes.<br><br><br>What is the lesson here? I believe the lesson is to use formats the are consistent all the way through, and conform things to those formats. For example, use a schema, use json, or anything that is known to be reliable. And put off using unreliable formats or process communication, etc for as long as possible. That includes strings, or anything that is meant for being read by humans, for example."
" <br>**What is the difference between ""Schema on Read"" and ""Schema on Write"" in databases?**"	<br><br>**Schema on Write:**<br>- Structure (schema) is defined before data is written to the database.<br>- Common in traditional relational databases (e.g., SQL databases).<br>- Requires data to conform to a predefined schema, ensuring data consistency and integrity.<br>  <br>**Schema on Read:**<br>- Data is stored in raw form, without a predefined schema.<br>- Schema is applied when data is read or queried.<br>- Common in NoSQL databases and big data environments (e.g., Hadoop, MongoDB).<br>- Offers flexibility to handle semi-structured or unstructured data, but can lead to more complex queries.

How do you stage modified and deleted files without including new, untracked files?	git add -u<br><br>Stages modified and deleted files that are already tracked by Git.<br><br>Does not stage new, untracked files.<br><br><br>Example:<br><br>1. Modify file1.txt and delete file2.txt.<br><br><br>2. Run git add -u.<br><br>Stages the modification of file1.txt and the deletion of file2.txt.<br><br>Leaves untracked files (e.g., new_file.txt) unstaged.<br><br><br><br>To also stage new, untracked files, use git add -A instead.

E.g.&nbsp;<code>--diff-filter=ad</code>&nbsp;excludes added and deleted paths.</div></div>

Why does pandas suck?	- It is an eager engine, meaning is executes operations immediately instead of waiting, like a lazy engine. This makes it difficult to optimize and make calculations in a smarter way.<br><br>- Missing values are set as NaN instead of NULL. <br>(This is because NumPy uses NaN and Pandas was built on top of NumPy.)<br>(If Pandas used Pythons native None to represent missing values, Pandas would have to store columns using the <b>object data type</b> (which is a generic type for mixed data). This is because None is not a numeric value<br><br>it is a Python object. This means it couldn't work with numeric data types, such as int64 and float64. Object types are stored as pointers to Python objects, which results in slower processing and higher memory usage.)<br><br>- When Pandas uses an object dtype, it sacrifices a lot of the performance benefits of working with numeric data types (like int64 or float64), which are much faster and more memory-efficient than object types. This is because:</div><br><br>- Numeric types are stored in contiguous memory blocks and can be processed by highly optimized numerical libraries.<br><br>- Object types, on the other hand, are stored as pointers to Python objects, resulting in slower processing and higher memory usage.<br><br>- primitive data types, such as int64 and float64, are lighter weight, stored contiguously in memory (as opposed to Objects, which are stored elsewhere, and the pointers to the memory holding these objects has to be stored and followed. They also don't have to be garbage collected like objects do.
Why would I ever want to use a technology like LibCST?	For python:<br><br>Is it similar to an AST (Abstract Syntax Tree), but is lossless. It keeps all indents, comments, etc.<br><br>It might be useful when trying to refractor a large python codebase, while maintaining formatting.<br><br><div><b>It is useful for building custom linters, codemods (</b><b>automated code refactoring tools</b>&nbsp;)<b>, or static analysis tools.</b></div>

Why is the following wrong/bad?<br><br>if (x instanceof Error) { ... }	It will provide a false negative with a cross-realm (such as iframes, or node's vm module.)<br><br>ou can't rely on instanceof Error because there are places where an error comes from where it will no longer be an Error instance.<br><br>You need a way to check if it's reliable.<br><br>There is a current javascript proposal that should fix this: error.isError()

What is an isokinetic movement?	A The term you're describing is isokinetic movement.<br><br>In isokinetic movements, the muscle's length remains constant during the movement because the joint angle or pivot point is changing, allowing for a consistent speed of motion. This is different from isometric contraction, where the muscle length stays constant because the joint does not move at all.<br><br>Specialized machines, such as isokinetic dynamometers, are often used to control the speed of movement while the muscle maintains consistent tension throughout the range of motion.<br><br>
the muscle's length remains constant during the movement because the joint angle or pivot point is changing, allowing for a consistent speed of motion.	Isokinetic movement

Type IIa (Fast Oxidative-Glycolytic)<br><br>Fast-twitch fibers<br><br>Moderate endurance and power<br><br>Use both aerobic and anaerobic metabolism<br><br><br><br>3. Type IIb (Fast Glycolytic)<br><br>Fast-twitch fibers<br><br>Low endurance, high power<br><br>Rely on anaerobic metabolism
[tech]<br><br>What 3 things does passing -- into the command line do?<br><br>Examples:<br>ls -- -l<br>yarn start -- --port 3000<br>echo -- --help	<div><b>1. Disambiguating Arguments</b></div><div><div><b>2. Passing Flags to Subcommands</b></div></div><div><div><b>3. Forcing Positional Arguments</b></div></div><div><b><br></b></div><div><b>Examples:<br><br>1.</b>&nbsp;<b>ls -- -l</b></div><div><div>  Without --: ls -l is interpreted as a flag to list files in long format.</div><br><div>  With --: ls -- -l treats -l as a filename, not a flag.</div></div><div><br></div><div><b>2.&nbsp;yarn start -- --port 3000</b></div><div><div> The first -- is for Yarn: It passes everything after it (--port 3000) as arguments to the script being executed.</div><br><div>  --port 3000 is then processed by the script as runtime arguments.</div></div><div><b><br></b></div><div><b>3.</b><b>&nbsp;echo -- --help</b><b><br></b></div><div><div>Output: --help (not interpreted as a flag, but printed as a string).</div></div>
"<img src=""FfimkIhtxehP6BxaNkH1AQ_5HY8XE1htHRIgWnHYgXDA_NuXmqNAKBw_Ligamentum_sacrotuberale_1.jpeg"">"	Sacrotuberous ligament

should I use git checkout?	if switching branches, use the newer command `git switch`

what is `git switch`?	A newer command that allows you to specifically checkout branches.
How do you refer the the commit before the latest one?	HEAD~
How do you switch to the previous commit?	git switch HEAD~
In git, what is the working directory?	the working tree,<br><br><div>the directory on your computer where you work with your projects files. It is the <b>current state of your files</b> that you can see, edit, and interact with directly.</div>
[git]<br><br><div>If your working directory and staging area have become messy (e.g., with many untracked or modified files), <b>this</b> command resets everything to match the last committed state.</div>	<div>git restore -WS .</div>
git: what do I use if I want to move a commit to another branch?	git cherry-pick
[git]<br><br>What are the steps to do a clean reset to match the remote?<br><br>(Use git restore)	1. git fetch origin<br>2. git restore -WS .<br>3. git clean -d --force<br>4. git switch --detach origin/main<br>5. git switch -C main<br><br>1)  Ensures youre working with the latest remote data.<br>2)  Discards all uncommitted changes and staged files, returning to the latest commit.<br>3)  Removes all untracked files and directories.<br>4)  Checks out the remote-tracking branch in a detached HEAD state, so you're pointing at exactly what's on the remote.<br>5)  Resets (or creates) your local main branch to match the current commit (ie origin/main), and switches to it.
"Difference in ""working copy"" in jj and git?"	in jj, a working copy automatically becomes a commit.

Say you are starting a new change:<br><br>What 4 steps do you do?	1. jj new<br>&nbsp; - run this when you are done with the previous change, and set things up for a new change.<br>2. jj describe&nbsp;<br>&nbsp; - this command lets you write what you are working on.<br>3. jj st&nbsp;<br>&nbsp; - this shows that status<br>4. (Change the files you want. Delete files, etc.)
If you already did `jj new` and want to make changes to the previous commit, what do you do?	Make your changes in the working copy, and then do a `jj squash`<br><br>jj squash squashes (moves) the changes from a given commit into its parent commit.<br><br>Note: this is similar to `git commit --amend`. In fact, `jj amend` is an alias for `jj squash`
jj equivalent of git commit --amend	jj squash
How do you edit an existing commit?	jj edit &lt;commit&gt;
How do you view how a change has evolved over time?	jj evolog

Difference between these 2:<br><br>jj new trunk()<br>VS<br>jj new main	"`jj new main` adds a new change to the end of the branch/trunk named ""main"".<br><br>`jj new trunk()` points to the current branch or trunk. You don't need to know the name."
revert to a commit 5 commits ago (using git log)	"<pre><code>git log<br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># use the arrow keys to scroll up and down in history</span><br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># once you've found your commit, save the hash</span><br>git revert [saved hash]<br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># git will create a new commit that undoes that commit</span><br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># follow prompts to edit the commit message</span><br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># or just save and commit</span></code></pre>"
[git]<br><br>Oh shit! You've accidentally commited to the wrong branch. (How to fix this not using cherry-pick?)	"<pre><code><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># undo the last commit, but leave the changes available</span><br>git reset HEAD~ --soft<br>git stash<br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># move to the correct branch</span><br>git checkout name-of-the-branch-the-commit-should-have-been-on<br>git stash pop<br>git add . # or add individual files<br>git commit -m <span style=""color: rgb(145, 158, 107);"">""your message here""</span>;<br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># now your changes are on the correct branch</span></code></pre>"
Oh shit, you've accidentally commited to the wrong branch? How do you fix this using cherry pick?	"<pre><code>git checkout name-of-the-branch-the-commit-should-have-been-on&gt;

git cherry-pick &lt;commit-hash|branch-name-where-commit-happened (to get most recent commit&gt;

git checkout &lt;branch-name-where-commit-happened&gt;</code></pre><pre><code>
git reset HEAD~ --hard</code></pre>"
what does HEAD~ mean?	It means the parent commit of the HEAD. So the previous commit before the most-recent one.
<pre><code>git reset HEAD~ --soft</code></pre><pre><code>vs</code></pre><pre><code>git reset HEAD~ --hard</code></pre>	--soft keeps all of changes in the staging area, and working directory in-tact.<br>--hard causes the staging area and working directory to also reset back to the previous commit.
[git]<br><br>What if you want to make one small change without a comment?<br>(Warning: don't do this if it has already been pushed up to a repo.)	"<pre><code><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># make your change</span><br>git add . # or add individual files<br>git commit --amend --no-edit<br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># now your last commit contains that change!</span><br><span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># WARNING: never amend public commits</span></code></pre>"
What if you've done something terribly wrong and want to see everything you've ever done so you can backtrack and go back in a time machine?	"<pre><code>git reflog
<span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># you will see a list of every thing you've</span>
<span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># done in git, across all branches!</span>
<span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># each one has an index HEAD@{index}</span>
<span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># find the one before you broke everything</span>
git reset HEAD@{index}
<span style=""color: rgb(153, 153, 153);""># magic time machine</span></code></pre>"
What is the difference between HEAD~ and HEAD~1?	Nothing

<script>
  // remove cloze syntax from revealed hint
  var hint = document.querySelector('.fullhint>[id^=""hint""]')
  var html = hint.innerHTML.replace(/\[\[oc(\d+)::(.*?)(::(.*?))?\]\]/mg, ""$2"")
  hint.innerHTML = html
  // scroll to cloze
  document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function() {
    setTimeout(function(){
      const cloze1 = document.getElementsByClassName(""cloze"")[0];
      const rect = cloze1.getBoundingClientRect();
      const absTop = rect.top + window.pageYOffset;
      const absBot = rect.bottom + window.pageYOffset;
      if (absBot >= window.innerHeight) {
        const height = rect.top - rect.bottom
        const middle = absTop - (window.innerHeight/2) - (height/2);
        window.scrollTo(0, middle);};
    }, 1);
  }, false);
</script>

"When debugging a shell script in vscode/cursor, the watch statement for debug_result is 'declare -- debug_result=""5""'<br><br>What if I want it to simply be '5'?"	Watch ${debug_result} instead of debug_result
[tech]<br>[bash]<br><br>When debugging a shell script in vscode/cursor and watching a variable that should be a 5 for example, but is intead an empty string '', or something like ')' or '()', what can I do?	"It could be a non-null separated string.<br><br>Or<br><br>Create a temp_result_file and echo the results to that file.<br><br>ie create a variable: local temp_result_file=""/tmp/calc_result.txt""<br>then: echo ""$result"" &gt; ""$temp_result_file""<br><br>then when debugging you can do the following:<br>debug_result=$(cat /tmp/calc_result.txt 2&gt;/dev/null)<br><br>Now setting a watch on ${debug_result} will hold the actual result in string form."
if watching ${debug_result} is '26', what will watching debug_result be?	"'declare -- debug_result=""26""'"
what does `exit 0` mean?	Success. No errors.
What does `echo $?` do?	$? stores the exit code of the last command.

([N] would mean ""normal"" mode. [V] would mean visual mode.)"

"[bash]<br><br><span style=""color: rgb(214, 214, 221);"">result=$(</span><span style=""color: rgb(239, 176, 128);"">eval</span><span style=""color: rgb(214, 214, 221);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(227, 148, 220);"">'fd -d 2 . . 2&gt;/dev/null'</span><span style=""color: rgb(214, 214, 221);"">)</span><br><br>When setting a watch variable on ${result} it just shows an empty string, even though echo ""$result"" shows something like the following:<br>./coverage/<br>./debug.sh<br>etc<br><br>Why? And what can be done about it?"	"Because $result is separated by \n new line characters, the watch variable doesn't support it. Bash squishes everything into one long string. So instead we have to replace the newline chars with something different.<br><br>For example, we can do the following:<br><div><div><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;"">debug_result=$(</span><span style=""color: #efb080;"">echo</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;""> </span><span style=""color: #e394dc;"">""</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;"">$result</span><span style=""color: #e394dc;"">""</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;""> | </span><span style=""color: #efb080;"">tr</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;""> </span><span style=""color: #e394dc;"">'\n'</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;""> </span><span style=""color: #e394dc;"">''</span><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;"">)</span></div></div><div><span style=""color: #d6d6dd;""><br></span></div><div>This will produce a result similar to the following:<br></div><div><img src=""Screenshot 2025-02-18 at 23.51.09.png""><br></div><div>(Note how these separators&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(227, 148, 220);"">&nbsp;</span>make it easy to select the results with the mouse.)</div><br><br><br>(""tr"" stands for transform or translate)"
Name of black lines in spectrography that indicates absorbed wavelengths where electrons absorb energy and jump up to a higher electron orbit, which can be used to detect which elements there are in stars?	Fraunhofer lines
Name of Science for studying the wavelength and colors of light?	Spectrography 
Bash scripting: What separators should be used?	\0 or \n
Why are \0 and \n typically the separators that should be used when handling data in a bash script?	\n is good for making things human-readable, and sometimes for debugging.<br>Sometimes the watch variables don't handle \0 null characters very well. So we might want to separate things with a \n. But sometimes that also fails, and so we might want to use a special character in that case.<br><br>But generally \0 null characters are well-handled (and sometimes \n newline characters), and so these should be generally used when you can. Something like pilcrow  can be used, it is generally not processed automatically by other scripts that you might want to pipe into.
[tech]<br><br>Unknown Errors in engineering, logic, programming..&nbsp;<br><br>What are they?	"<img src=""programming-unknown_errors-anki.jpg"">"
[tech]<br><br>Types of programming errors.<br><br>Think Cells	"<img src=""cells-programming-errors-33ce76b0203860e909a13b747880fb781fc7ba61.jpg"">"
[tech]<br><br>What are the main types of boundary conditions / boundary errors?	- Algorithm Boundaries<br><br>- Logical Boundaries, ie transitions btwn states<br><br>- Resource Limits (This is related to boundary conditions because if a system is overly taxed, at its limit and lacking in resources then it will encounter boundary errors. A way to test a system is to push it to its limit, and also to limit its resources. For example, how does the skin and other prote tive barriers behave when nutrition and immune cells are limited?)<br><br>- Concurrency Issues<br><br>- Mathematical Boundaries<br><br>- Edge Cases<br><br>- Junctions or Communications between two systems
[tech]<br><br>What kind of Unaccounted / Accounting Errors might you encounter?	Configuration Drift<br>Data Packet Loss<br>Misconfiguration<br>Misalignment<br>Resource Not Found<br>State Management Errors<br>Type Errors<br><br><pre><br>CD MM RST<br><br>Configuring Data or Misconfiguring and Misaligning Resources, State Management and Type Errors.<br><br>Imagine: <br><br>M&Ms are used by a fat accountant.<br>He is old and uses CDs and has a Nintendo.<br>You throw a CD at his M&Ms, and it lands into his Nintendo, and he presses reset because he's retarded.<br></pre>
What is a Serialization Error?	When data cannot be correctly converted into a format for storage or transmission.<br><br>Serialization is the process of converting objects into different formats so that they can be saved or sent over networks or to other systems.
[tech]<br><br>What is the difference between Serialization and Mashalling?	Marshalling is more general. <br>It is more often used for preparing input/outputs (function args and return values) for remote execution.<br>An example: RPC (Remote Procedure Calls).<br><br>Serialization: <br><b>object  format</b> <br>(ie saving JSON to disk).<br><br>Marshalling:<br><b>object  network-ready format</b> <br>(ie often for RPC).
What is a Contract Violation?	<div>when a system <b>fails to adhere to an agreed-upon interface, schema, or protocol</b>.</div>
[tech]<br><br>What is an Integration Error?	<div><b>Integration Error</b></div><br><div><br></div><br><div>An <b>integration error</b> occurs when two or more systems, services, or components <b>fail to interact correctly</b> due to mismatches in data, protocols, or expectations. These errors typically arise when different modules, APIs, or external dependencies are integrated into a larger system.</div>
[tech]<br><br>What is a IPC Error?	<div><b>IPC (Inter-Process Communication) Error</b></div><br><div><br></div><br><div>An <b>IPC (Inter-Process Communication) Error</b> occurs when <b>two or more processes fail to exchange data correctly</b>. IPC is used in <b>multi-threaded</b> or <b>multi-process</b> applications, distributed systems, and OS-level process management.</div>
What are the main causes of IPC Errors?	"<div><b>Causes of IPC Errors</b></div>
<div> 1. <b>Shared Memory Conflicts</b></div>
<div>  Two processes try to <b>write to the same memory</b> at the same time, causing corruption.</div>
<div>  <b>Fix</b>: Use <b>mutexes (locks)</b> or <b>semaphores</b>.</div>
<div> 2. <b>Deadlocks in Message Passing</b></div>
<div>  Process A <b>waits for</b> Process B, while Process B <b>waits for</b> Process A, creating an infinite block.</div>
<div>  <b>Fix</b>: Implement <b>timeout mechanisms</b> and <b>deadlock detection algorithms</b>.</div>
<div> 3. <b>Queue Overflows</b></div>
<div>  If one process sends <b>too many messages</b>, the queue <b>fills up</b>, causing message loss.</div>
<div>  <b>Fix</b>: Use <b>bounded queues</b> or <b>backpressure mechanisms</b>.</div>
<div> 4. <b>Serialization Issues</b></div>
<div>  One process <b>sends</b> data in a format another process <b>cant deserialize</b>.</div>
<div>  <b>Fix</b>: Ensure both processes <b>use the same serialization format</b> (e.g., JSON, Protocol Buffers).</div>
<div> 5. <b>Process Lifecycle Mismatch</b></div>
<div>  One process <b>expects another process to be running</b>, but it has already exited.</div>
<div>  <b>Fix</b>: Implement <b>graceful error handling</b> and <b>process monitoring</b>.</div>"

<div><b>What is a WSGI Server?</b><b></b></div>	A python web application cannot handle HTTP requests directly from production web servers.<br><br>So a WSGI server is middleware that sits between a python web app and a web server.<br><br>It allows web servers like Nginx, Apache, Caddy, to communicate with python-based web apps like Flask, Django, FastAPI
If you are creating a python web app that uses Flash, Django, FastAPI, and also want to use a web server such as Nginx or Apache or Caddy, what else do you need?	Middleware: WSGI (Web Server Gateway Interface)
